        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.6b03757"><meta property="og:title" content="Electrospun TiO2/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/acsami.6b03757.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Nanosized TiO2 is now actively developed as a low-cost and potentially high capacity anode material of Na-ion batteries, but its poor capacity utilization and insufficient cyclability remains an obstacle for battery applications. To overcome these drawbacks, we synthesized electrospun TiO2/C nanofibers, where anatase TiO2 nanocrystals with a diameter of ∼12 nm were densely embedded in the conductive carbon fibers, thus preventing them from aggregating and attacking by electrolyte. Due to its abundant active surfaces of well-dispersed TiO2 nanocrytals and high electronic conductivity of the carbon matrix, the TiO2/C anode shows a high redox capacity of ∼302.4 mA h g–1 and a high-rate capability of 164.9 mAh g–1 at a very high current of 2000 mA g–1. More significantly, this TiO2/C anode can be cycled with nearly 100% capacity retention over 1000 cycles, showing a sufficiently long cycle life for battery applications. The nanofibrous architecture of the TiO2/C composite and its superior electrochemical performance may provide new insights for development of better host materials for practical Na-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/acsami.6b03757.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931886d84bb454b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886d84bb454b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'ed039abd-4af5-4ba4-ac27-ebc789641afc'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886d84bb454b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="931886d84bb454b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Electrospun TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Nanofibers As a High-Capacity and Cycle-Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b03757", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">121</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/medium/am-2016-037578_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b03757&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Nanosized TiO<sub>2</sub> is now actively developed as a low-cost and potentially high capacity anode material of Na-ion batteries, but its poor capacity utilization and insufficient cyclability remains an obstacle for battery applications. To overcome these drawbacks, we synthesized electrospun TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers, where anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals with a diameter of ∼12 nm were densely embedded in the conductive carbon fibers, thus preventing them from aggregating and attacking by electrolyte. Due to its abundant active surfaces of well-dispersed TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrytals and high electronic conductivity of the carbon matrix, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode shows a high redox capacity of ∼302.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a high-rate capability of 164.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a very high current of 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. More significantly, this TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode can be cycled with nearly 100% capacity retention over 1000 cycles, showing a sufficiently long cycle life for battery applications. The nanofibrous architecture of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C composite and its superior electrochemical performance may provide new insights for development of better host materials for practical Na-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D16684%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYa%2BXiong%252C%2BJiangfeng%2BQian%252C%2BYuliang%2BCao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D26%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b03757%26title%3DElectrospun%2BTiO2%252FC%2BNanofibers%2BAs%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%2Band%2BCycle-Stable%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D16689%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tio2%2Fc+Nanofibers&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_TiO2%2FC%20nanofibers">TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrospinning+Synthesis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long+Cycle+Life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long%20cycle%20life">long cycle life</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Low cost and long life rechargeable batteries are now in great demand for ever-increasing electric storage applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> Though lithium-ion batteries have successfully used for powering electric vehicles and tested for distributed power stations, their large-scale applications is still a serious concern about the high costs and limited resources of Li reserves on earth. In fact, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have similar electrochemical performance but potentially much lower cost to their lithium counterparts because of the widespread availability of sodium resources, thus making them a competitive candidate for electric storage applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4, 5)</a> In recent years, a great effort has been devoted to find suitable materials for Na-storage reaction and a variety of cathodic hosts, such as transition-metal oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6-8)</a> polyanionic phosphates <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9-11)</a> and Prussian blue analogues, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> have been revealed to deliver acceptable Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion capacity with certain cyclability. In contrast, only few of anode materials are reported to have decent Na-storage performance up to data. Despite a few of hard carbons were revealed to have considerable Na-storage capability, their low potential and large polarization are a severe concern for battery application. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Quite a large array of alloy compounds such as Sn, Sb and their sulfides and phosphides have shown high reversible capacities for Na-storage anodes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 14 to 19">(14-19)</a> However, the structural instability due to their large volume change during charge–discharge cycles is still a big obstacle as in the case of Li-insertion electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20, 21)</a> Therefore, it is still a challenge to search for low-cost and cycling-stable anode materials for development of practically viable SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>), a low-cost, structurally stable, and environmentally benign anode material, has been recently proved to be a promising Na(Li)-insertion anode with adequately high reversible capacity and good cyclability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22-24)</a> Several polymorphs of TiO<sub>2,</sub> such as amorphous, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25, 26)</a> anatase, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27-29)</a> rutile, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> TiO<sub>2</sub>(B), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32, 33)</a> and TiO<sub>2</sub> (H), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> have been proposed as anode hosts for SIBs; however, these materials exhibited a large diversity in the electrochemical Na- storage performance. Recently, Wang et al. reported electrochemical properties of different phases of TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres and found that anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> has a better Na-storage capability than mixed anatase/rutile or amorphous TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Several groups have combined the nanoarchitectural design with conductive composite engineering to improve the capacity utilization of TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36, 37)</a> However, most of the nanostructured TiO<sub>2</sub>/C composites reported so far have a limited reversible capacity less than 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref38" aria-label="reference 35 and 38">(35, 38)</a> and only a few of them can deliver a higher capacity of 250 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at low rates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39, 40)</a> Nevertheless, all the pioneering works seem to suggest that one-dimensional nanosized anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> is advantageous for achieving higher capacity utilization and rate capability as a Na-insertion anode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 41 to 43">(41-43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we report the synthesis of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>/carbon nanofibers (TiO<sub>2</sub>/C) through an electrospinning process and the electrochemical performance of this material as a Na<sup>+</sup> insertion anode. Due to the one-dimensional confinement of nanosized TiO<sub>2</sub> in carbon nanofibers, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers has a shortened Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path and the enhanced electronic conductivity, which greatly facilitate Na-insertion reaction. Meanwhile, the nanofiber morphology also offers abundant electrochemically active surface sites, enabling a high utilization of the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. As expected, The TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode exhibits a high capacity of 302.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with excellent capacity retention of ∼100% over 1000 cycles, offering a promising alternative anode for constructing low-cost and high-capacity Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A single-nozzle electrospinning technique was employed to synthesize the TiO<sub>2</sub>/carbon nanofibers. The precursor solution for electrospinning process was prepared by dissolving 1.7 g of tetrabutyl titanate (Ti(OC<sub>4</sub>H<sub>9</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, 97%,) and 0.8 g of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP, Mw = 1 300 000, Aldrich) in 9.2 g ethanol (≥99.9%) at vigorous stirring for 12 h. Next, the precursor solution was loaded into a 10 mL syringe equipped with a size nine needle and the flow rate of solution was set to 8.5 mL h<sup>–1</sup> by a syringe pump (Model LSP01–1A, Shanghai, China). A high-voltage power supply (model DW-N503–1A CDF, Tianjin, China) was connected to the needle in order to apply a voltage of 10 kV between the needle and the collecting aluminum plate for the formation of nanofibers. Heat-treatment of the as-collected nanofibers was carried out in a tube furnace at 550 °C in Ar atmosphere for 3 h at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.)</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Structural Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, ZEISS Merlin Compact) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL, JEM-2010-FEF) were employed to characterize the morphologies of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers. The crystalline structure of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers was evaluated by X-ray diffraction spectrometry (XRD) on a Shimadzu XRD-6000 machine. Thermogravimetric measurement (TGA) was conducted on a TGA Q500 thermogravimetric analyzer (TA Instruments, USA). The X-ray photoelectron spectrometry was used to identify the valence states of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C electrodes cycled at different charge/discharge depths using a Thermo Scientific spectrometer (ESCLAB 250 Xi) equipped with an Al Kα achromatic X-ray source (1486.68 eV).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode, we gently mixed 80 wt % TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers, 10 wt % super P, and 10 wt % poly(acrylic acid)  (PAA, 25 wt %) to form a slurry, and then the slurry was coated onto a copper foil to form an anode film. After being dried under vacuum at 80 °C for 12 h, the anode film was cut into small disks with 1 cm diameter. The loading weight of the active material in the anode film was 1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The CR2016-type half cells were assembled in a glovebox filled with highly pure argon gas (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O levels &lt;0.5 ppm) by sandwiching a glass-fiber separator (Whatman) between the anode film and a metallic sodium disk and then filling an electrolyte solution of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC)/ethylene carbonate (EC) (PC:EC = 1:1, in volume). Electrochemical performances of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode were evaluated by galvanostatic charge/discharge on a LAND cycler (Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co., China) and cyclic voltammetry on a CHI 660c electrochemical workstation (ChenHua Instruments Co., China).)</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The preparation process and synthetic chemistry of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers are schematically illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/suppl_file/am6b03757_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. In the electrospinning process, the precursor solution was extruded from the syringe needle under electrostatic drive to form the nanofiber precursor, which was converted into the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C composite nanofibers at subsequent heat treatment through the thermal carbonization of PVP polymer and simultaneous hydrolysis of Ti(OC<sub>4</sub>H<sub>9</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. On the basis of the weight losses in TGA measurement (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a), the total TiO<sub>2</sub> content in the composite was determined to be 81%, which is in agreement with the expected value from complete carbonization and hydrolysis reactions of the precursor materials.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/medium/am-2016-037578_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Physical characterizations of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers: (a) TGA curve, (b) XRD spectra, (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm, and (d) pore size distribution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b03757&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The structural and morphological features of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers were examined by XRD, TGA, SEM and TEM spectrometry. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, all the XRD peaks can be indexed to the (101), (200), (105)/(211), and (204) diffractions of a tetragonal phase, corresponding to anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 021–1272), space group <i>I</i>4<sub>1</sub>/<i>amd</i>, <i>a</i> = 3.785 Å, and <i>c</i> = 9.514 Å). The weak intensities of the XRD signals were attributed to the nanosized TiO<sub>2</sub> embedded in amorphous carbon matrix and no any diffraction was detected from other phases, suggesting the high purity of the as-synthesized TiO<sub>2</sub> crystallites. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms were measured todetermine the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area and pore size distribution, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, d. The BET surface area and the average pore width of TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers were determined to be 15.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 14.7 nm, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–d shows the morphologies of the electrospun TiO<sub>2</sub>/C fibers after the thermal treatment. In the SEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, b), the sample appears as well-dispersed but mutually connected nanofibers with a uniformly distributed diameter of ∼120 nm, forming a fibrous network. The surfaces of the nanofibers are very rough with densely distributed TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, which may lead to high active surface areas for the Na-insertion reaction. The EDX element mapping image in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/suppl_file/am6b03757_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> indicates that both carbon and TiO<sub>2</sub> are homogeneously distributed in the composite nanofibers. In the magnified TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c), it is clearly visualized that the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles have an average size of ∼12 nm and are dispersed throughout the carbon fibers. The lattice fringes in the high-resolution TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) has a spacing of 0.35 nm, corresponding to the (101) planes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>. The amorphous regions around the lattice fringes reflect the disordered carbon phase.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/medium/am-2016-037578_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphological characterization of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers: (a, b) SEM images; (c) TEM image; and (d) high-resolution TEM image.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b03757&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Na insertion performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode were studied by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements. As demonstrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, a large and broad cathodic band appears at the potential region of 1.8 to 0 V during first negative scan, which considerably decreases its intensity during subsequent cycles, suggesting that the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) by decomposition of electrolyte was involved in the initial sodiation reaction of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers. At subsequent scans, the pair of cathodic and anodic current bands kept their shapes and intensities almost unchanged, implying stable and reversible redox reactions of the material. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, cycled at a constant current of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage interval of 3.0–0.01 V. Similar to its CV features, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode displays sloped charge/discharge profiles without distinct plateaus, suggesting a homogeneous Na insertion into the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice. The TiO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode shows initial charge/discharge capacities of 391/227.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a Coulombic efficiency of 58% at the first cycle. This low Coulombic efficiency was also frequently encountered for the nongraphite Li<sup>+</sup>-insertion anodes due to the formation of a SEI film by the electrolyte decomposition and this problem can be well solved by a prelithiation technique for practical battery applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> During subsequent cycles, the reversible capacity remained stably at 212 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, showing a remarkable cycling stability. Taking into account of the possible capacity contributions of the carbon components to the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode, we also measured the Na-storage capacities of the carbon fibers and conductive super P carbon powder and found that these carbons contributed only a few tens of milliampere hours (∼31.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/suppl_file/am6b03757_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) in the total capacity. Nevertheless, the Na-insertion capacity of the TiO<sub>2</sub> crystallites in the anode reached to 302.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a moderate rate of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 1 Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into per TiO<sub>2</sub> unit. This capacity is possibly the highest among the TiO<sub>2</sub> materials for Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion anodes.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/medium/am-2016-037578_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical behaviors of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofiber anode: (a) CV curves scanned at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) charge/discharge profiles measured at a constant current of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) cycling performance at a constant current of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; and (d) rate capability at changing current rates from 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b03757&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers also exhibit remarkable long-term cyclability and high-rate performance. As displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode can deliver a high capacity of 240.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a quite high current of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after a few cycles of initial activation and then keep this capacity almost constant at 237.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 1000 cycles, suggesting a very reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reaction in the structurally stable TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice. To provide further evidence for the structural stability of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers, SEM and TEM images of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers were taken from the cycled electrode, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/suppl_file/am6b03757_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. It can be seen from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/suppl_file/am6b03757_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> that the fibrous morphology of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers remains intact after 150 and 200 cycles and is still distinguishable even after 1000 cycles, indicating a strong tolerance of the material to the volumetric change during Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion/extraction cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate performances of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers are further evaluated at various charge–discharge rates from 50 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, the reversible capacity is slightly decreased from 254.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> to 241.3, 227.7, and 203.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with increasing current rates from 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to100, 200, and 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even when the current rate was raised up to very high rates of 1000 and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, this anode can still remain 185.3 and 164.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, indicating a rapid reaction process for Na<sup>+</sup>- insertion process. This superior rate performance is apparently ascribed to the nanoarchitectural design of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C composite, where the well-dispersed TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles provide abundant active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion and meanwhile their surrounding carbon matrix offers high electric conduction paths for fast electron transport.</div><div class="NLM_p">To confirm the Na-insertion mechanism, we disassembled the test cells and characterized the structural changes of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anodes cycled at different states of charge/discharge by ex situ XRD technique. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the XRD patterns of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anodes charged and discharged at different depths. During the discharge process, all the XRD peaks kept their positions and decreased their intensities with increasing the depth of discharge, as expected from a Na-insertion reaction. When fully discharged to 0.01 V, the whole XRD pattern became indistinct, representing an amorphous state of the sodiated TiO<sub>2</sub>. This progressive amorphourization reaction is obviously brought about by the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ion into the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice during discharge, which leads to a decrease of the lattice symmetry of the material. Once reversely charged to 3 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), the XRD peaks reemerged at their original positions with a slightly weakened intensities, demonstrating a highly reversible structural change during charge/discharge reactions. Since no traceable XRD signals from Ti<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>O phases could be detected, the charge/discharge mechanism of the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanofibers can only be assigned to Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion/extraction reactions. To further confirm this reaction mechanism, we calibrated the changes in the oxidation state of Ti element by XPS analysis of the cycled TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anodes. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode shows two XPS peaks at 458.9 and 464.6 eV, corresponding to the binding energies of Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p 3/2 and Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p 1/2, respectively. When discharged to 0.01 V, these binding energies negatively shifted to 457.8 and 463.4 eV, respectively, suggesting that all the Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions in the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice were reduced to Ti<sup>3+</sup> ions. After a reversed charge to 3.0 V, the binding energies recovered to their original values, indicating a reversible conversion of Ti<sup>3+</sup> into Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions in the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice. This XPS evidence combining with XRD analysis reveal a completely chemical and structural reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion reaction in the TiO<sub>2</sub> host.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/medium/am-2016-037578_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Left: Typical charge/discharge profile of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofiber anode, on which arabic characters denote the sampling potential, right: XRD patterns of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofiber anode at different charge/discharge depths; (b) Ti 2p spectra of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofiber anode at (a) open circuit and (d) discharged to 0.01 V and (g) recharged to 3 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/asset/images/large/am-2016-037578_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b03757&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To test its practical feasibility for battery applications, we assembled a full Na-ion battery using the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode coupled with a Na-rich Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode. The construction and electrochemical performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> full cell are described in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b03757/suppl_file/am6b03757_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. As expected from the potential difference between the TiO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> couple, this full Na-ion battery give an open circuit voltage of 3.2 V and an average discharge voltage of &gt;2.0 V. Derived from the charge/discharge curves of the full cells, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anode can deliver a discharge capacity of 235.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, fully utilizing its reversible capacity in a practical Na-ion battery.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers were successfully synthesized by a single-nozzle electrospinning method followed with subsequent heat treatment. Benefiting from a combination of the large active surfaces of well-dispersed TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrytals and the high electronic conductivity of the carbon fibers, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofiber electrode demonstrated a high reversible capacity of ∼302.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a strong rate capability with 164.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a very high current of 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Particularly because of being dispersively embedded in the carbon matrix, the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals were prevented from aggregating and attacking by electrolyte, therefore exhibiting a remarkably long-term cycling stability with nearly 100% capacity retention over 1000 cycles. Such superior electrochemical performances described above make the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C nanofibers a promising anode material for constructing low-cost and high-capacity Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2338-2360</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.                 10.1039/c2ee02781j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D5884-5901%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DV.%2BPalomares%26author%3DP.%2BSerras%26author%3DI.%2BVillaluenga%26author%3DK.%2BB.%2BHueso%26author%3DJ.%2BCarretero-Gonzalez%26author%3DT.%2BRojo%26title%3DNa-Ion%2BBatteries%252C%2BRecent%2BAdvances%2Band%2BPresent%2BChallenges%2Bto%2BBecome%2BLow%2BCost%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2BSystems%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc2ee02781j&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b03757&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc2ee02781j&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, M. H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gonzalo, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Singh, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rojo, T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Comprehensive Review of Sodium Layered Oxides: Powerful Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">8</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">81</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">102</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4EE03192J</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D81-102%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DM.%2BH.%2BHan%26author%3DE.%2BGonzalo%26author%3DG.%2BSingh%26author%3DT.%2BRojo%26title%3DA%2BComprehensive%2BReview%2Bof%2BSodium%2BLayered%2BOxides%253A%2BPowerful%2BCathodes%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC4EE03192J&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b03757&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC4EE03192J&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">81-102</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Deng, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Routes to High Energy Cathodes of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 cation-disordered electrode for high-rate symmetric rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yuesheng; Xiao, Ruijuan; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Avdeev, Maxim; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides exhibit Na+/vacancy-ordered superstructures because of strong Na+-Na+ interaction in the alkali metal layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer.  These superstructures evidenced by voltage plateaus in the electrochem. curves limit the Na+ ion transport kinetics and cycle performance in rechargeable batteries.  Here we show that such Na+/vacancy ordering can be avoided by choosing the transition metal ions with similar ionic radii and different redox potentials, for example, Cr3+ and Ti4+.  The designed P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 is completely Na+/vacancy-disordered at any sodium content and displays excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  A sym. sodium-ion battery using the same P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 electrode delivers 75% of the initial capacity at 12C rate.  Our contribution demonstrates that the approach of preventing Na+/vacancy ordering by breaking charge ordering in the transition metal layer opens a simple way to design disordered electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE01365H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Talaie, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Duffort, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Smith, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A combination of operando X-ray diffraction, pair distribution function (PDF) anal. coupled with electrochem. measurements and M.ovrddot.ossbauer spectroscopy elucidates the nature of the phase transitions induced by insertion and extn. of sodium ions in P2-Na0.67[NiyMn0.5+yFe0.5-2y]O2 (y = 0, 0.10, 0.15).  When phase transitions are avoided, the optimal cathode material - P2-Na0.67Fe0.2Mn0.65Ni0.15O2 - delivers 25% more energy than the unsubstituted material, sustaining high specific energy (350 Wh kg-1) at moderate rates and maintains 80% of the original energy d. after 150 cycles - a significant improvement in performance vs. the unsubstituted analog.  The crystal structure of the high voltage phase is solved for the first time by X-ray PDF anal. of P2-Na0.67-zFe0.5Mn0.5O2 (where z ∼ 0.5), revealing that migration of the transition metals - particularly Fe3+ - into tetrahedral sites in the interlayer space occurs at high potential.  This results in new short range order between two adjacent layers.  Although the transition metal migration is reversible as proven by electrochem. performance, it induces a large disfavorable cell polarization.  The deleterious high voltage transition is mitigated by substitution of Fe3+ by Mn4+/Ni2+, giving rise to better cycling performance.  Moreover, as demonstrated by 57Fe M.ovrddot.ossbauer spectroscopy, the much lower ratio of Fe4+O6 to Fe3+O6 obsd. systematically across the range of Ni content - compared to the values expected from a purely ionic model - suggests redox activity involves the O-2p orbitals owing to their overlap with the transition metal-3d orbitals.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201401946"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Feng, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Honeycomb-Layered Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>: A High-Rate and Cycle-Stable Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Stannous sulfide@reduced graphene oxide (SnS@RGO) composite is successfully synthesized via a facile pptn. route.  The structural and morphol. characterizations reveal SnS@RGO composites are composed of SnS nanoparticles of the size 5-10 nm, which are uniformly anchored on the surface of RGO. Compared to the neat SnS nanoparticles, the enhanced electrochem. performance of the SnS@RGO composite is primarily due to the incorporation of RGO as a highly conductive, flexible component as well as possessing a large available surface area, which provides desirable properties such as improved electronic contact between active materials, aggregation suppression of intermediate products, and alleviation of the vol. change during sodiation and desodiation.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb is a promising high-capacity anode material in Na-ion batteries, but it generally shows poor cycling stability because of its large vol. changes during Na ion insertion and extn. processes.  To alleviate or even overcome this problem, we developed a hybrid C encapsulation strategy to improve the anode performance of Sb through the combination of Sb/N-doped C (Sb/NC) hybrid nanostructures and the C nanotube (CNT) network.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Well-organized C nanotube (CNT)@TiO2 core/porous-sheath coaxial nanocables were synthesized by controlled hydrolysis of tetra-Bu titanate in the presence of CNTs - these were studied with SEM, TEM, XRD and electrochem. expts.  The CNT@TiO2 coaxial nanocables show excellent rate capability and cycling performance compared with both pure CNT and pure TiO2 when used as anode materials for Li-ion batteries (LIBs).  Both the sp. capacity in the CNT core and that in the TiO2 sheath are much higher than that of the TiO2-free CNT and that of the CNT-free TiO2 sample, resp.  The coaxial cable morphol. provides a soln. to the ionic-electronic wiring problem in LIBs as well as the synergism of the 2 cable wall materials.  The CNT core provides sufficient electrons for the storage of Li in TiO2 sheath, but the CNT itself can also store Li whereby this storage kinetics is, in turn, improved by the presence of the nanoporous TiO2 because the thin protection layer on TiO2 (unlike free CNT) enables rapid access of Li-ions from the liq. electrolyte. We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase TiO2 nanoparticles for high power sodium-ion anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Liming; Buchholz, Daniel; Bresser, Dominic; Gomes Chagas, Luciana; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
